Emancipation by Addressing a Slave as 'Free'
Ruling with talil on nidā' effecting emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that calling a slave 'Ya hurr' or 'Ya atiq' effects emancipation and explain the talil based on the nature of nidā' with a sarīh word.

Core Idea
Calling a slave 'Ya hurr' implies emancipation because the call's reality asserts the truth of the description.

In Hanafi law, words can effect emancipation without explicit intention if they are sarīh (explicit) in meaning. This lesson examines the unique case where a master addresses his slave as 'Ya hurr' (O free one) or 'Ya atiq' (O freed one). The ruling is that the slave becomes free. We will unpack the linguistic reasoning behind this ruling and understand how the reality of nidā' leads to this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verbal act and its ruling
The master says 'Ya hurr' or 'Ya atiq' to his slave. The ruling is that the slave becomes free (ya'tiqu). This is the effect without any additional intention.
The ruling is based on the assumption that the speaker is the owner of the slave.
2
Understand what makes these words 'sarīh' for emancipation
The words 'hurr' (free) and 'atiq' (freed) are explicit (sarīh) in meaning emancipation. When used in a call (nidā'), they are not just descriptive but performative: the act of calling someone by a description implies that the description is true.
For a word to be sarīh, it must be unambiguous in its legal effect.
3
Analyze the linguistic principle (haqīqah al-nidā')
The reality of a call (nidā') is to bring the person to mind by mentioning a description (waṣf). When the master calls the slave 'Ya hurr', he is summoning him under the attribute of 'free', which presupposes that the attribute is actually present. Since the master has authority over the slave's status, his call is a confirmation of freedom from his side.
The call itself is a declaration, not just a request.
4
Conclude the legal effect
Because the master's call implies the existence of the attribute of freedom, and he is the one who can grant freedom, the call effects emancipation. The slave becomes free by the very act of the address.
This is a form of kinayah (implied expression) that is still effective because of the strength of the implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال له ياحر يا عتيق يعتق لأنه نداء بماهو صريح فى العتق وهو لاستحضار المنادى بالوصف المذكور هذا هو حقيقته فيقتضى تحقق الوصف فيه وانه يثبت من جهته فيقتضى ثبوته تصديقا له فيما اختر و سنقرره
And if he says to him, 'Ya hurr' or 'Ya atiq', he becomes free, because it is a call with something explicit in emancipation, and it is to bring the called person to mind by the mentioned description; this is its reality. So it necessitates the realization of the description in him, and that it is established from his (the caller's) side. So it necessitates its establishment as a confirmation for him in what he has chosen, and we will decide on it.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with talil (reasoning). It addresses the case where a master calls his slave 'Ya hurr' (O free one) or 'Ya atiq' (O freed one). The ruling is that the slave becomes free (ya'tiqu). The reasoning is that this is a call (nidā') using words that are explicit in emancipation (sarīh fī al-‘itq). The essential nature (haqīqah) of such a call is to summon the person using a description (waṣf). When the master calls the slave 'ya hurr', he is summoning him by the description 'free', which implies that the slave actually possesses that attribute. Since the master is the one who has authority over the slave's status, this implies that the master is confirming the attribute of freedom from his side. Therefore, the call itself necessitates the realization of freedom. The commentator notes that this will be discussed further.

Key Terms
نداء (nidā'): A call or address using the vocative particle 'yā'.
صريح (sarīh): Explicit, unambiguous in meaning. Used here for words that are directly indicative of emancipation.
عتق (‘itq): Emancipation of a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave does not become free; it is just an address.
B) The slave becomes free because it is a call with an explicit word for emancipation.
C) The slave becomes free only if the master intended emancipation.
D) The slave becomes a mudabbir.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, calling a slave 'Ya hurr' or 'Ya atiq' effects emancipation because the linguistic reality of the call implies the truth of the attribute of freedom.

Explicit Words of Emancipation: No Need for Intention
States the ruling and reasoning for explicit emancipation phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on explicit emancipation words and distinguish between sarih and kinayah, applying the diyanatan/qadan distinction.

Core Idea
Sarih words of emancipation free the slave even without intention, as legal custom suffices.

This passage addresses a fundamental Hanafi principle: explicit words (sarih) in emancipation create the legal effect immediately, regardless of the speaker's inner intention. The author explains that these words have become so established in legal and customary usage that they function as performative declarations (insha'), not merely informative statements (khabar). Understanding this rule requires distinguishing between what is accepted in conscience (diyanatan) and what is enforced in court (qadan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that uttering any of the listed explicit phrases frees the slave, whether the master intended emancipation or not.
The phrases are: 'ant hurr', 'mu'taq', 'atiq', 'muharrar', 'qad harrartuka', 'qad a'taqtuka'.
2
Examine the reasoning (dalil aqli)
The reason given is that these words are sarih (explicit) because they are used in legal and customary contexts to mean emancipation. This obviates the need for intention and linguistic origin.
The proof is based on 'urf' (custom) and the principle that legal necessity transforms informative statements into performatives.
3
Understand the transformation from khabar to insha'
The author notes that originally these words were informative (khabar) but, out of necessity in legal transactions, they are treated as performative (insha') similar to divorce and sale.
This transformation is a key Hanafi concept for transactions requiring immediate legal effect.
4
Apply the diyanatan vs qadan distinction
If the master claims he meant something else (false report or free from work), he is believed in conscience (diyanatan) but not in court (qadan) because the apparent meaning contradicts his claim.
Diyanatan: private conscience; qadan: court enforcement. This protects the master's sincerity while upholding legal certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اذا قال لعبده اوامته انت حر او معتق او عتیق او محرر اوقد حررتک اوقد اعتقك فقد عتق نوى به العتق اولم ينو لان هذه الالفاظ صريح فيه لانها مستعملة فيه شرعا و عرفا فاغنى ذالك عن النية والوضع وان كان في الاخبار فقد جعل انشاء في التصرفات الشرعية للحاجة كما في الطلاق والبيع و غيرهما ولو قال عنيت به الاخبار الباطل او انه حر من العمل صدق ديانة لانه يحتمله ولا يدين قضاء لانه خلاف الظاهر
And if he said to his male slave or his female slave: 'You are free' or 'emancipated' or 'freed' or 'liberated' or 'I have freed you' or 'I have emancipated you', then he has been emancipated, whether he intended emancipation by it or did not intend, because these words are explicit in it, because they are used in that meaning legally and customarily, so that sufficed from intention and linguistic origin. And even if they were originally informative, they have been made performative in legal transactions out of necessity, as in divorce, sale, and others. And if he said, 'I meant by it a false report' or 'that he is free from work', he is believed as a matter of conscience because it is possible, but he is not believed in court because it contradicts the apparent.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on explicit words of emancipation. The author, commenting on al-Hidayah, explains that uttering any of six specific phrases—such as 'ant hurr' (you are free)—immediately frees the slave, regardless of the master's intention. The reason is that these words are considered 'sarih' (explicit) because they are used both legally and customarily for this purpose. They originally were informative (khabar), but out of necessity for legal transactions, they have been treated as performative (insha'), similar to words of divorce and sale. The passage also addresses a nuance: if the master later claims that he intended to inform falsely or meant 'free from work', he is believed in his conscience (diyanatan) because the words can bear that meaning, but the court (qadan) does not accept this because it contradicts the apparent meaning. This diyanatan vs qadan distinction is a hallmark of Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit, unambiguous words that require no intention for their legal effect.
إنشاء (inshāʼ): A performative utterance that creates a legal effect, as opposed to informative (khabar).
ديناً (diyānatan): In conscience; privately accepted as true based on the inner state.
قضاءً (qaḍāʼan): In court; juridically enforced based on apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) انت حر
B) معتق
C) محرر
D) انت طالق

Key Takeaway
Explicit words of emancipation (sarih) free the slave without need for intention, but a claim of alternative meaning is accepted only in conscience, not in court.

Ruling on Calling a Slave 'Ya Hurr' or 'Ya Atiq'
Ruling with rational proof on emancipation via call.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on calling a slave by an emancipation-explicit attribute and distinguish when it effects emancipation versus when it is merely a proper-name call.

Core Idea
Calling by an attribute of freedom frees; calling by a proper name does not.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle linguistic scenario: when a master calls his slave using words that explicitly denote freedom, such as 'O free one' or 'O emancipated one'. According to Hanafi jurists, such a call generally effects emancipation. However, an important exception occurs when the slave is actually named or nicknamed with that word, shifting the purpose from describing to identifying. Understanding this distinction protects the rights of both master and slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a master calling his slave using words like 'Ya Hurr' or 'Ya Atiq'. According to the author, this results in the slave's emancipation.
The ruling is that the slave becomes free.
2
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is based on the nature of the call: calling by an attribute that is explicit in emancipation implies that the attribute is true of the person called. Since the master has authority to free, his utterance is taken as a declaration of freedom.
This is a rational (ʿaqlī) proof, not a scriptural one.
3
Recognize the exception for proper names
If the slave's actual name or nickname is 'Hurr' and the master calls him 'Ya Hurr', it does not effect emancipation because the intent is to summon by name, not to describe.
The word 'Hurr' here functions as a proper noun (ism ʿalam).
4
Apply the rule to the Persian example
If the master calls the slave in Persian 'Azad' (free) while his nickname is 'Hurr', emancipation occurs because the call is not by his proper name; the attribute is reported, not the name.
The reverse also holds: calling by a proper name in a different language does not change the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال له ياحر يا عتيق يعتق لأنه نداء بما هو صريح في العتق وهو لاستحضار المنادى بالوصف المذكور هذا هو حقيقته فيقتضى تحقق الوصف فيه وانه يثبت من جهته فيقتضى ثبوته تصديقا له فيما اختر و سنقرره من بعد ان شاء الله تعالى الا اذا سماه حرا ثم ناداه يا حر لان مراده الاعلام باسم علمه وهو ما لقبه به و لو ناداه بالفارسية يا آزاد و قد لقبه بالحر قالوا يعتق و كذا عكسه لانه ليس بنداء باسم علمه فيعتبر الاخبار عن الوصف
And likewise if he said to him: 'O free one, O emancipated one', he is emancipated because it is a call with that which is explicit in emancipation, and it is for summoning the one called by the mentioned attribute; this is its reality. So it requires the attribute to be realized in him, and that it is established from his side. So it necessitates its establishment in confirmation of what he chose. And we will elaborate hereafter, God willing. Except if he named him 'Hurr' then called him 'O Hurr', because his intent is notification by his proper name, which is his nickname. And if he called him in Persian 'O Azad' while he had nicknamed him 'Hurr', they (the scholars) said he is emancipated, and likewise the reverse, because it is not a call by his proper name, so consideration is given to the report about the attribute.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a master calls his slave using words like 'Ya Hurr' (O free one) or 'Ya Atiq' (O emancipated one). According to the author, such a call effects emancipation. The reasoning is that calling someone by an attribute that is explicit in emancipation implies that the attribute is true of the person being called. Since the master has the authority to give that attribute, his utterance is treated as a declaration of freedom. However, an important exception is made: if the slave's actual name is 'Hurr' (or he is nicknamed so), and the master calls him by that name, it does not effect emancipation because the master's intention is simply to summon him by his name, not to assert the attribute. Conversely, if the master calls him in Persian 'Azad' (free) while his nickname is 'Hurr', emancipation occurs because the call is not by his proper name. This ruling highlights the Hanafi principle that the apparent meaning of the utterance is decisive, and it distinguishes between calling by an attribute versus calling by a proper name.

Key Terms
نداء (nidāʼ): A call or summons that brings the addressee to mind by a specific description.
وصف (waṣf): An attribute (such as freedom) used to address the slave.
اعلام (iʻlām): Notification or making known by a proper name.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Emancipation Through Mentioning a Body Part
Ruling that mentioning a part implies emancipation of the whole slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master attaches emancipation to an inseparable body part of a slave, and explain the rationale based on customary usage (urf).

Core Idea
Mentioning an inseparable body part in emancipation frees the entire slave.

This short passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario: what happens if a master says 'Your head is free' or 'Your face is free' to his slave? The ruling is straightforward—the entire slave becomes free. The reasoning is that such terms, by custom, refer to the whole body. The author also reminds us that the same principle applies in divorce law, and that there is a difference of opinion to be discussed later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The master makes a declaration of emancipation by saying 'your head is free' or similar. The legal effect is that the entire slave becomes free, not just the mentioned part.
The act is a valid declaration of emancipation.
2
Understand the principle of 'inseparable part' (juz' sha'i')
An inseparable part is one that cannot be removed without destroying the whole. In Hanafi fiqh, when a legal action (like divorce or emancipation) is attached to such a part, the action applies to the whole. This is based on customary usage (urf).
Examples: head, face, neck, body, private part.
3
Apply to the examples given
The passage lists 'your head', 'your face', 'your neck', 'your body', and for a female slave 'your private part'. All these are considered parts that idiomatically represent the whole, so emancipation is complete.
The same rule applies to any inseparable part.
4
Note the upcoming ikhtilaf
The author indicates that there is a difference of opinion among scholars (to be discussed later). The Hanafi position is as stated, but other schools may have different views, e.g., requiring explicit mention of the whole.
Be aware that this is the settled Hanafi ruling, but valid ikhtilaf exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كذا لو قال راسك حر او وجهك او رقبتك او بدنك او قال لامته فرجك حر لان هذه الالفاظ يعبر بها عن جميع البدن وقد مر في الطلاق و ان اضافه الى جزء شائع يقع في ذلك الجزء و سيأتي الاختلاف فيه ان شاء الله تعالى
And likewise if he said: 'Your head is free' or 'your face' or 'your neck' or 'your body', or he said to his female slave: 'Your private part is free', because these words are used to express the entire body, and it has already passed in divorce that if he adds it to a common part, it falls upon that part, and the difference of opinion will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This brief passage states the ruling that if a master says something like 'Your head is free' or 'Your face is free' (or any similar part), or says to his female slave 'Your private part is free', the entire slave becomes free. The reason given is that these words are idiomatically used to refer to the entire body. The author reminds us that the same principle was already discussed in the chapter on divorce: when an action is attached to an inseparable part (juz' sha'i'), it affects the whole. The passage also indicates that there is a difference of opinion among scholars on this matter, which will be discussed later. This ruling is straightforward: any mention of a part essential to the whole (like the head, face, neck) results in total emancipation.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' shāʼiʻ): An inseparable part of a thing, such that its mention implies the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, attaching emancipation to an inseparable body part (like head, face, neck, body, private part) results in the entire slave becoming free, based on customary usage.

Calling a Slave 'Ya Hurr': Implicit Emancipation
Ruling on calling slave with freedom-descriptive words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on calling a slave by freedom-descriptive words and identify the exception when the word is the slave's name.

Core Idea
Calling a slave by a freedom word frees him, unless it is his name.

This lesson examines a fascinating case where the linguistic structure of a simple call can produce a legal effect. The Hanafi school holds that when a master calls his slave using a word like 'free' or 'emancipated', the very act of calling implies that the attribute is realized, and since only the master can grant freedom, the slave becomes free. However, an important exception exists: if the word is the slave's actual name or nickname, the call is purely identificational and has no emancipatory effect. This ruling illustrates how the Hanafi school weighs linguistic implications and intention in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master calling his slave with a descriptive word of freedom (e.g., 'Ya Hurr' or 'Ya Atiq'). The Hanafi ruling is that the slave becomes free.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the linguistic nature of nida (calling): calling someone with an attribute implies that attribute is present in the called person. Since freedom is within the master's power, it must be established to confirm the master's statement.
The reasoning is based on customary understanding of nida, not on a direct textual source.
3
Identify the exception
If the word of freedom is actually the slave's name or nickname, then the call is mere identification, not a description, so the slave does not become free.
The master's intent is to call by name, not to declare emancipation.
4
Apply the exception to the Persian case
If the master calls in Persian (e.g., 'Ya Azad') while the slave's nickname is 'Hurr', the call is not by his name, so the descriptive effect applies and the slave is freed. The same holds if the reverse.
Language does not affect the ruling; only whether the word is used as a name matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مولیٰ نے مملوک سے کہا یا حرا یا عتیق تو وہ مملوک آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ پکارنا ایسے لفظ کے ساتھ ہے۔ جو عشق میں صریح ہے اور ندا ، منادی کو وصف مذکور کے ساتھ حاضر کرنے کیلئے ہوتا ہے اور یہ منادی کے حقیقی معنی ہیں۔ پس تقاضا کرتا ہے کہ منادی میں یہ وصف ثابت ہو اور یہ وصف پکارنے والے کی طرف سے ثابت ہو سکتا ہے تو مولی کے قول کی تصدیق کے واسطے اس وصف کا ثابت ہونا لازم آیا اور ہم آئندہ انشاء اللہ اس کی تقریر کریں گے۔ لیکن اگر مولی نے اس کا نام در رکھا ہو۔ پھر اس کو پکارا کہ اے حر ( تو آزاد نہ ہوگا ) کیونکہ مولیٰ کی مراد یہ ہے کہ اس کے نام سے اس کو خبر دار کرے۔ یعنی جو اس کا لقب رکھا ہے اور اگر اس کو فارسی میں پکارا کہ اے آزاد اور اس کا لقب حر رکھا تھا۔ تو مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اسی طرح اگر اس کے برعکس ہو کیونکہ یہ اس کے نام سے ندا نہیں ہے ۔ پس اعتبار کیا جائے گا اخبار عن الوصف کا۔
If the master says to the slave 'O free one' or 'O emancipated one', then that slave becomes free. Because this calling is with a word that is explicit in emancipation, and calling (nida) is to present the called one with the mentioned attribute, and this is the real meaning of calling. So it necessitates that this attribute is established in the called one, and this attribute can be established from the caller's side. So for the sake of confirming the master's statement, the establishment of this attribute in the called one (the slave) becomes necessary. And we will elaborate this later if Allah wills. But if the master had named him 'Hurr' (free), then he called him 'O Hurr', then he will not become free, because the master's intention is to alert him by his name, i.e., the nickname he has given him. And if he called him in Persian 'O Azad' while his nickname was 'Hurr', the scholars said that he becomes free. Similarly if the reverse, because this is not calling by his name, so it will be considered as a report about the attribute.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It discusses the effect of calling a slave by words that describe freedom. The key principle is that when a master calls his slave using a descriptive word like 'free' or 'emancipated', the very act of calling implies that the description is real, because nida (calling) is to present the addressed person with a certain attribute. Since the attribute of freedom can only be established by the master's own action (emancipation), the slave becomes free. However, an exception arises when the slave's actual name or nickname is that word (e.g., his name is 'Hurr'). In that case, the master's intent is simply to call him by name, not to declare freedom. Therefore, the slave does not become free. Similarly, if the master uses a different language (Persian 'Azad') while the nickname is 'Hurr', the calling is not by the name, so the descriptive effect applies and the slave is freed. The same holds if the reverse. This shows the importance of intent and linguistic usage in determining legal effects.

Key Terms
نداء (nida): Calling or summoning someone with a specific attribute, which linguistically implies that the attribute is real in the called person.
وصف (wasf): An attribute or description used in calling, such as 'hurr' (free) or 'atiq' (emancipated).
حر (hurr): Free person; used as a descriptive word for emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave becomes free because the word 'Hurr' is explicit for freedom.
B) The slave does not become free because the master is just calling him by his name.
C) The slave becomes free only if the master intended emancipation.
D) The slave becomes free only if the call is in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants emancipation through implicit declaration (kinayah) when a master calls his slave by a freedom-descriptive word, provided the word is not the slave's name.

What Frees the Whole Slave: Metonymic Expressions in Hanafi Emancipation Law
Ruling and proof for metonymic emancipation expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that expressions like 'your head is free' effect full emancipation, and explain the proof based on customary usage of these terms.

Core Idea
Saying 'your head is free' frees the entire slave — because these terms refer to the whole body.

This lesson examines a set of expressions that, although literally naming a specific body part, are traditionally understood as referring to the entire person. The Hanafi school rules that such statements result in complete emancipation of the slave. We will explore the proof for this and also introduce the concept of 'common part' (juz' sha'i') emancipation, which follows a different rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expressions at issue
Note the list: 'your head is free', 'your face', 'your neck', 'your body', and for a female slave 'your genitals are free'. These are the specific phrasings that trigger full emancipation.
The text includes body (بدن) and genitals (فرج) as whole-body references.
2
Understand the proof (wajh al-dalalah)
The author states that these expressions 'refer to the entire body' (يعبر بها عن جميع البدن). This is the core proof: linguistic convention (urf) treats these terms as metonymic for the whole person.
The same principle was established in the Book of Divorce (كتاب الطلاق) — cross-reference to that chapter.
3
Distinguish from juz' sha'i' (common part)
If the master says 'one-third of you is free' that is a common part (جزء شائع), not a specific limb. The ruling differs: emancipation first applies to that fraction, then spreads. This will be detailed later.
The text hints at an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this point.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding expressions that, although referring to a specific body part, are conventionally used to denote the entire body. The Hanafi position is that saying 'your head is free' or 'your face is free' etc. results in full emancipation because these terms are metonymic for the whole person. The same applies to saying 'your genitals are free' to a female slave. The reasoning is that these expressions are commonly used to refer to the whole body, as established in the chapter on divorce. The passage also introduces the concept of a 'juz' sha'i'' (common part like a fraction) where emancipation first applies to that fraction and then spreads to the whole. This is distinct from a specific part like hand or foot, which will be discussed in the next lesson.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway

Assigning Emancipation to a Specific Limb: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi vs. Shafi'i on freeing a specific limb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on assigning emancipation to a specific limb and explain the analogy with divorce that supports it.

Core Idea
Freeing a hand does not free the slave in Hanafi law; only expressions that conventionally denote the whole body effect emancipation.

This passage addresses a classic inter-madhhab dispute. The question is whether declaring a specific part of a slave's body 'free' — such as saying 'your hand is free' — results in the full emancipation of the slave. The Hanafi school, represented by Abu Hanifa and his disciples, says no, drawing an analogy with divorce. The Shafi'i school, along with some other imams, says yes. Understanding this ruling hinges on the concept of conventional usage (urf) in legal language.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the act and its ruling according to the Hanafi school. The act is declaring a specific limb free; the ruling is that no emancipation occurs.
The text says 'at us' (indana) indicating Hanafi consensus.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school (and also Imam Zufar and Imam Ahmad) holds that the slave becomes fully free.
The passage lists 'al-Shafi'i' and the Urdu adds Imam Zufar and Imam Ahmad.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their reasoning is that freeing a part implies freeing the whole, as the part cannot be separated from the whole.
This is based on a different understanding of the implication of the declaration.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response uses qiyas (analogy) to divorce: just as saying 'your hand is divorced' does not effect divorce, so too 'your hand is free' does not effect emancipation, because these limbs are not conventionally used to denote the whole.
The author says 'the discussion is like that in divorce and we have explained it'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in Hanafi law the slave remains enslaved; in Shafi'i law the slave becomes free.
This affects inheritance, contracts, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اضافه الى جزء معين لا يعبر به عن الجملة كاليدو الرجل لا يقع عندنا خلافا للشافعي والكلام فيه كالكلام في الطلاق و قد بيناه
ترجمہ اور اگر آزاد کرنا کسی معین جز کی طرف منسوب کیا جس سے پورا بدن تعبیر نہیں کیا جاتا ہے جیسے ہاتھ ، پاؤں تو ہمارے نزدیک آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ امام شافعی کا اختلاف ہے اور اس میں کلام ایسا ہے جیسا طلاق میں اور اس کو ہم بیان کر چکے۔
تشریح مسئلہ ! اگر آزاد کرنا کسی ایسے جز معین کی طرف منسوب کیا جائے جس سے پورے بدن کو تعبیر نہیں یا جاتا۔ مثلاً ہاتھ کی طرف منسوب کیا یا پاؤں کی طرف تو اس صورت میں ہمارے نزدیک آزادی واقع نہیں ہو گی۔ البتہ امام شافعی ، امام زفر اور دامام احمد کے نزدیک اس صورت میں بھی آزادی واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اس بارے میں تفصیلی کلام باب ایقاع الطلاق میں بیان کیا جا چکا۔ اعادہ کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔
And if he assigns it to a specific part that is not used to denote the whole, like the hand or foot, it does not take effect according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The discussion here is like that in divorce, and we have explained it. Urdu translation: And if emancipation is assigned to a specific part that does not express the whole body, like hand, foot, then according to us emancipation does not occur. Imam al-Shafi'i differs. And the discussion is like that in divorce, and we have explained it. Explanation: If emancipation is assigned to a specific part that does not express the whole body, e.g., hand or foot, then according to us emancipation does not occur. However, according to Imam al-Shafi'i, Imam Zufar, and Imam Ahmad, emancipation occurs even in this case. The detailed discussion has been given in the chapter on divorce; repetition is not needed.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The issue is whether saying 'your hand is free' or 'your foot is free' results in the emancipation of the entire slave. The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that it does not, because these limbs are not conventionally used to denote the whole body. The reasoning is analogous to divorce: if a man says to his wife 'your hand is divorced', it does not effect divorce. The Shafi'i school (and also Imam Zufar and Imam Ahmad) holds that it does result in emancipation, because freeing a part implies freeing the whole. The Hanafi position is based on the principle that such expressions are not recognized as legally effective for full emancipation. The text refers to the chapter on divorce for a detailed analogy.

Key Terms
جزء معين (juz' mu'ayyan): A specific part of the body that is not used metonymically for the whole, such as a hand or foot.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; the analogy in this passage is to the ruling that declaring divorce on a limb is ineffective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master says to his slave 'Your hand is free', what is the ruling?
A) The slave becomes fully free.
B) Only the hand becomes free.
C) No emancipation takes effect.
D) It depends on the master's intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only emancipation expressions that conventionally denote the whole body are valid; partial declarations like 'your hand is free' have no effect.

Ambiguous Expressions and Intention in Emancipation
Ruling on ambiguous phrase with intention for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'I have no ownership over you' and explain why intention (niyyah) is necessary for its legal effect.

Core Idea
An ambiguous expression requires intention to give it legal effect; without niyyah, no emancipation occurs.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling statement from Al-Hidayah concerning an ambiguous phrase (kinayah). The master says to his slave, 'I have no ownership over you.' This phrase could either mean a sale or an emancipation. The Hanafi school holds that the legal outcome depends solely on the master's intention. If he intends freedom, the slave is free; otherwise, the slave remains enslaved. We will explore why intention is decisive and how this principle applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Conditions
Read the passage and note the two scenarios: with intention and without intention. Identify what the ruling is in each case.
2
Recognize the Ambiguity
Understand that the phrase 'I have no ownership over you' is kinayah (indirect). It has at least two possible meanings: sale or emancipation. The text explicitly mentions these possibilities.
Kinayah cannot create a legal effect without intention.
3
Determine the Role of Intention
Intention (niyyah) is the factor that resolves the ambiguity. Without it, neither meaning is specified, and the phrase has no legal consequence.
Intention is an internal act; it is not required to be spoken.
4
Apply the Ruling to a Hypothetical
Imagine a master says the phrase with no intention. What is the outcome? Now imagine he intends freedom. Confirm that the ruling aligns with the principle.
The same rule applies to other ambiguous statements in Hanafi law, such as in divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لا ملک لی علیک ونوى به الحرية عتق وان لم ينولم يعتق لانه يحتمل انه اراد لا ملک لی علیک لانی بعتك ويحتمل لانى اعتقتك فلا يتعين احد هما مرادا الابالنية
ترجمہ اور اگر ( مملوک سے ) کہا میری تجھ پر کوئی ملک نہیں ہے اور اس سے آزادی کی نیت کی تو آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اگر نیت نہ کی تو آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ اسلئے احتمال ہے کہ قائل کی مراد یہ ہو کہ میری تجھ پر کوئی ملک نہیں ہے۔ اس واسطے کہ میں نے تجھے بیچ ڈالا اور (یہ بھی ) احتمال ہے کہ میں نے تجھ کو آزاد کر دیا ۔ پس بغیر نیت کے کوئی احتمال متعین نہیں ہوگا۔
And if he says 'I have no ownership over you' and intends by it freedom, the slave becomes free; and if he does not intend, he does not become free, because it is possible that he meant 'I have no ownership over you because I sold you' and it is possible that he meant 'because I freed you'. So neither meaning is determined except by intention.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding an ambiguous phrase (kinayah). The phrase 'I have no ownership over you' is not explicit for emancipation; it could mean sale or freedom. Therefore, the slave's status depends on the master's intention. If the master intends emancipation by this phrase, the slave becomes free. If he does not intend emancipation, the slave remains enslaved. This principle applies because ambiguous expressions require niyyah (intention) to specify their legal effect. The text highlights that the phrase is susceptible to multiple possibilities, so without intention, no effect.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart that specifies the meaning of an ambiguous expression.
کنایہ (kinayah): An indirect or ambiguous expression that requires intention to determine its legal effect.

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Expressions Describing the Whole Body in Emancipation
Explanation of words like 'your private part is free' leading to full emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on expressions like 'your head is free' and explain the linguistic basis for full emancipation.

Core Idea
Expressions describing a part idiomatically referring to the whole effect full emancipation in Hanafi fiqh.

In this passage, the commentator explains that if a master says to his slave 'your head is free' or similar phrases, the entire slave becomes free. The key insight is that such words are used figuratively for the entire person, as established in the Book of Divorce. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the linguistic reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expressions and the ruling
The text lists words like 'your head is free', 'your face is free', 'your neck is free', 'your private part is free'. The ruling: the slave becomes fully free.
These are all examples of juz' shayi' — a part that describes the whole.
2
Understand the linguistic proof
The proof is that these words are conventionally used to express the entire body. This is supported by analogy to similar rulings in the Book of Divorce.
The proof is based on linguistic 'urf (custom) and qiyas (analogy).
3
Distinguish the case of a fractional share
If emancipation is attributed to a fraction (like one-third), it first takes effect in that fraction then spreads. The text notes an upcoming disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on this point.
This distinction is important for understanding the scope of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: Your private part is free. Because these are words by which the entire body is expressed, and this has been mentioned in the Book of Divorce. And if emancipation is attributed to a part that describes the whole body (juz' shayi'), then emancipation occurs in that part and spreads to the whole body. The disagreement regarding this will come soon, God willing.
Explanation of the issue: If the master says to his male slave: 'Your head is free', or 'Your face is free', or 'Your neck is free', or 'Your body is free', or to his female slave: 'Your private part is free' - then the slave becomes free by all these words. The proof is that all these words are those by which the whole body is expressed, and this has been mentioned in the Book of Divorce. Refer to it. And if emancipation is attributed to a part that describes the whole body, like one-third, one-fourth, then first emancipation occurs in that part, then it spreads to the whole body. There is a disagreement between Imam (Abu Hanifa) and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding this. God willing, it will come soon.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage explains a ruling from the Book of Emancipation regarding expressions that refer to the whole body. The commentator states that if a master says to his slave 'your private part is free,' or similar expressions like 'your head is free,' 'your face is free,' 'your neck is free,' or 'your body is free,' the slave becomes fully free. The reasoning is that these expressions, though referring to a part, are used idiomatically to describe the entire body. This is analogous to similar rulings in the Book of Divorce. The passage also mentions that if emancipation is attributed to a fraction of the slave (like one-third or one-fourth), it first takes effect in that fraction and then spreads to the rest of the body. The commentator notes that there is a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on this point, which will be discussed later.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' shayi'): A part that describes the whole body, such as head, face, neck, etc., which are used to refer to the entire person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The entire slave becomes free

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, expressions like 'your head is free' effect full emancipation because they are conventionally used to refer to the entire person, not just the body part mentioned.

Kinayah in Emancipation: The Case of 'La Mulka Li Alayka'
Explanation that 'I have no ownership over you' requires intention for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a kinayah expression in emancipation and state the Hanafi requirement of intention (niyyah) for its legal effect.

Core Idea
A kinayah expression requires intention; without it, no legal effect occurs.

When a master says 'I have no ownership over you' to his slave, the legal consequence depends on his intention. Is this an emancipation? Or does it imply a sale? The Hanafi school classifies this as a kinayah, a metaphorical expression that is ambiguous and thus requires an explicit intention to be effective. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression
Recognize the phrase 'la mulka li alayka' as a potential kinayah. Note its ambiguity: it could mean either emancipation or sale.
The phrase is not explicit like 'anta hurr' (you are free).
2
Understand the Hanafi classification
The Hanafi school classifies such expressions as kinayah because they admit more than one possible meaning. For kinayah, intention is required to determine the intended meaning.
This is based on the principle that ambiguous words need additional evidence.
3
Apply the ruling
If the master intends emancipation, the slave is freed. If no intention or other intention (like sale), emancipation does not occur.
The burden is on the master's intention, which can be proven by his statement or circumstances.
4
Consider the proof (dalil)
The proof is that the text itself shows the phrase can mean two things. The Hanafis rely on the principle that without intention, the ambiguity remains unresolved.
The opposing view (Shafi'i) may classify it as explicit in certain contexts, but the Hanafis hold to the requirement of intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لا ملک لی علیک ونوى به الحرية عتق وان لم ينو لم يعتق لانه يحتمل انه اراد لا ملک لی علیک لانی بعتك ويحتمل لانى اعتقتك فلا يتعين احد هما مرادا الا بالنية
ترجمہ اور اگر ( مملوک سے ) کہا میری تجھ پر کوئی ملک نہیں ہے اور اس سے آزادی کی نیت کی تو آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اگر نیت نہ کی تو آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ اسلئے احتمال ہے کہ قائل کی مراد یہ ہو کہ میری تجھ پر کوئی ملک نہیں ہے۔ اس واسطے کہ میں نے تجھے بیچ ڈالا اور (یہ بھی ) احتمال ہے کہ میں نے تجھ کو آزاد کر دیا ۔ پس بغیر نیت کے کوئی احتمال متعین نہیں ہوگا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مولی نے اپنے مملوک سے کہا لا ملک علیک اور اس کلام سے آزاد کرنے کی نیت کی تو یہ مملوک آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اگر آزادی کی نیت نہیں کی تو آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ کلام الفاظ کنایات میں سے ہے کیونکہ اس میں دو احتمال ہیں ایک یہ کہ میری ملک تجھ پر اسلئے نہیں کہ میں نے تجھے بیچ ڈالا اور دوسرا احتمال یہ ہے کہ ملک اس واسطے نہیں کہ میں نے تجھے آزاد کر دیا۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ یہ کلام آزاد کرنے کے معنی میں صریح نہیں ہے بلکہ کنائی ہے اور لفظ کنائی عمل میں نیت کا محتاج ہوتا ہے۔ اس کے بغیر نیت کے کوئی احتمال متعین نہیں ہو گا اور آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔
And if he says, 'I have no ownership over you' and intends by it freedom, then he becomes free; and if he does not intend, he does not become free, because it is possible that he meant 'I have no ownership over you because I sold you,' and it is possible that he meant 'because I freed you.' So neither one is determined as intended except by intention.
Translation: And if (to the slave) he says, 'I have no ownership over you' and intends freedom, then the slave becomes free; and if he does not intend, he does not become free. Because there is a possibility that the speaker meant 'I have no ownership over you because I sold you,' and there is also a possibility that he meant 'I freed you.' So without intention, neither possibility is determined.
Explanation of the case: If the master says to his male slave 'la mulka alayka' and intends emancipation by these words, then the slave becomes free. If he does not intend emancipation, then emancipation does not occur. The proof is that these words are among the expressions of kinayah (metaphorical) because they have two possibilities: one that I have no ownership because I sold you, and the other that I have no ownership because I freed you. So it is established that this utterance is not explicit in the meaning of emancipation but is kinayah, and a kinayah expression requires intention for its operation. Without intention, no possibility is determined, and emancipation does not occur.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific expression of kinayah (metaphorical expression) for emancipation: when the master says 'la mulka li alayka' (I have no ownership over you). The commentator explains that this phrase is not explicit in meaning; it could mean either that the slave was sold or that the slave was freed. Therefore, emancipation only occurs if the master intends freedom. If he does not intend, the slave remains enslaved. The reasoning is that since the word is ambiguous, it requires explicit intention to be effective. This principle applies generally to all kinayat expressions in the context of emancipation.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kinayah): A word or phrase that is not explicit in its meaning but can be interpreted in multiple ways, requiring intention for a specific legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The slave becomes free after a court ruling

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a kinayah expression like 'I have no ownership over you' requires an explicit intention of emancipation to be effective; without it, the slave remains enslaved.

Kinayah Expressions for Emancipation: Intention Required
List of kinayah expressions for emancipation and their ruling requiring intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the kinayah expressions for emancipation listed by al-Quduri and explain why intention is necessary, distinguishing them from explicit expressions like divorce.

Core Idea
For kinayah expressions of emancipation, intention is decisive: ambiguity requires niyyah.

This lesson examines the kinayah expressions used for freeing slaves as outlined by al-Quduri. These phrases are ambiguous—they could imply emancipation, sale, or kitabah. Because of this ambiguity, the master's intention is critical: without it, no emancipation occurs. We will analyze each expression, understand the underlying rationale, and explore a special case narrated from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
List the kinayah expressions
Identify each phrase mentioned by al-Quduri: 'You have come out of my ownership', 'I have no way to you', 'I have no right of ownership over you', 'I have set you free', and 'I have released you' (atlaqtuk).
These are the standard kinayah for emancipation in the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the ambiguity
For each expression, consider the alternative legal meanings: they could also refer to sale (bay') or kitabah (contract of manumission). Because of this ihtimal (possibility), the expression is not explicit.
The ambiguity is the key reason intention is required.
3
Apply the intention requirement
If the master utters a kinayah expression with the intention of emancipation, the slave is freed. Without intention, no legal effect occurs. The burden is on the master to prove intention if disputed.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Note the special case of 'atlaqtuk'
The phrase 'atlaqtuk' (I have released you) is treated as a kinayah equivalent to 'khallaytu sabilak'. This is narrated from Abu Yusuf and requires intention.
Even though it sounds explicit, it remains kinayah.
5
Distinguish from explicit expressions
The word 'tallaqtuk' (I have divorced you) is explicit for divorce and cannot effect emancipation, even with intention. It is not a kinayah for 'itq.
Divorce and emancipation are legally distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عتق کے الفاظ کنائی کا حکم
قال وكذا كنايات العتق وذلك مثل قوله خرجت من ملكي ولاسبيل لى علیک و لارق لی علیک وقد خلیت سبیلک لانه يحتمل نفى السبيل والخروج عن الملك و تخلية السبيل بالبيع او الكتابة كما يحتمل بالعتق فلابد من النية وكذا قوله لامته قد اطلقتك لانه بمنزلة قوله خليت سبیلک و هو المروی عن ابي يوسف بخلاف قوله طلقتك على مانبين من بعد ان شاء الله تعالى
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا۔ یہی حال ہے کنایات عتق کا اور یہ جسے مولیٰ کا قول تو میری ملک سے نکل گئی اور میرے واسطے تجھ پر کوئی راہ نہیں ہے اور میرے لئے تجھ پر رقیت نہیں ہے اور میں نے تیری راہ چھوڑ دی۔ کیونکہ احتمال ہے کہ بیع کرنے یا مکاتب کرنے سے راہ خالی کرنا اور ملک سے نکالنا ( مراد ہو ) اسی طرح یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ آزاد کرنے سے (ایسا کیا ہو ) پس نیت ضروری ہے اور یونہی (اگر ) مولی نے اپنی باندی سے کہا کہ میں نے تجھ کو چھوڑ دیا۔ کیونکہ یہ خلیت سبیلک کے مرتبہ میں ہے اور یہی ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے۔ بخلاف اس کے (اگر) اس نے ( باندی سے ) کہا میں نے تجھے طلاق دے دی۔ چنانچہ ہم آئندہ انشاء اللہ بیان کریں گے۔
تشریح صاحب قدوری نے فرمایا کہ یہی حال آزاد کرنے کے الفاظ کنایہ کا ہے۔ یعنی الفاظ کنایہ سے اگر آزاد کرنے کی نیت کی ہے تو آزادی واقع ہو جائے گی ورنہ نہیں الفاظ کنایہ کی مثال جیسے اپنے غلام سے کہا تو میری ملک سے نکل گیا۔ یا باندی سے کہا تو میری ملک سے نکل گئی اور مثلاً کہا میرے واسطے تجھ پر کوئی راہ نہیں ہے۔ یا تجھ پر میری رقیت نہیں ہے۔ یا میں نے تیری راہ خالی کر دی ، چھوڑ دی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ ان تمام الفاظ میں آزادی کے علاوہ دوسرے معنی کا بھی احتمال ہے۔ کیونکہ جس طرح یہ معنی ہے کہ میں نے آزاد کر دیا۔ اسلئے تو میری ملک سے نکل گیا یا میرے آزاد کرنے کی وجہ سے میری ملک تجھ پر نہیں رہی وغیر ذالک۔ اسی طرح یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ چونکہ میں نے تجھ کو بیچ ڈالا یا مکاتب کر دیا اسلئے تو میری ملک سے نکل گیا یا میری ملک تجھ پر نہیں رہی ۔ پس ایک احتمال متعین کرنے کیلئے نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے اور اگر نیت نہیں پائی گئی تو آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی اور یوں ہی اگر اپنی باندی سے کہا کہ میں نے تجھ کو اطلاق کیا یعنی چھوڑ دیا تو بھی نیت ضروری ہے کیونکہ قد اطلقک، خلیت سبیلک کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ یہی امام ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے۔ اسکے برخلاف اگر مولی نے اپنی باندی سے کہا، میں نے تجھے طلاق دی تو اس صورت میں نیت کے باوجود آزادی ثابت نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ یہ لفظ طلاق میں صریح ہے۔ اسلئے اس سے آزادی ثابت نہیں ہوگی ۔ چنانچہ ہم آئندہ ان شاء اللہ بیان کریں گے۔
Ruling on kinayah expressions of emancipation.
He (al-Quduri) said: And similarly are the kinayah expressions of emancipation, such as his saying: 'You have come out of my ownership', 'I have no way to you', 'I have no right of ownership over you', 'I have set you free'. Because it is possible that the denial of way, exit from ownership, and setting free could be by sale or kitabah, just as it is possible by emancipation. So intention is necessary. Similarly, if he says to his slave-girl 'I have released you' (atlaqtuk), because it is like saying 'khallaytu sabilak', and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Unlike his saying 'I have divorced you' (tallaqtuk), as will be explained later, God willing.
Translation: Al-Quduri said: The same applies to kinayah expressions of emancipation. These include the master's words: 'You have come out of my ownership', 'I have no way to you', 'I have no right of ownership over you', 'I have set you free'. Because it is possible that the negation of way, exit from ownership, and setting free could occur by sale or kitabah (contract of manumission), just as it is possible by emancipation. Therefore, intention is necessary. Similarly, if the master says to his slave-girl 'I have released you' (atlaqtuk), because it is of the same rank as 'khallaytu sabilak', and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Unlike if he says 'I have divorced you' (tallaqtuk) to his slave-girl, as will be explained later.
Explanation: Imam al-Quduri said that the same applies to the kinayah expressions of emancipation. That is, if from a kinayah expression he intends emancipation, then emancipation occurs; otherwise not. Examples include saying to a male slave: 'You have left my ownership' or to a female slave: 'You have left my ownership', or saying 'I have no way to you', or 'I have no right of ownership over you', or 'I have set you free'. The proof is that all these words have a possible meaning other than emancipation, because just as these words could mean that I freed you, so too they could mean that I sold you or made a kitabah contract with you. Therefore, to determine one meaning, intention is necessary; if intention is not found, emancipation does not occur. Similarly, if he says to his slave-girl 'I have released you' (atlaqtuk), intention is necessary, because 'atlaqtuk' is of the same rank as 'khallaytu sabilak'. This is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. In contrast, if the master says to his slave-girl 'I have divorced you', then emancipation is not established even with intention, because this word is explicit in divorce and does not apply to emancipation. This will be explained later.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive list of kinayah (metaphorical) expressions that can be used for emancipation, but only if the master has the intention of freeing the slave. The commentator, drawing from al-Quduri's text, explains that expressions like 'you have left my ownership', 'I have no way to you', 'I have no right of ownership over you', and 'I have set you free' are all ambiguous because they could also refer to sale or kitabah. Therefore, intention is necessary. A specific case is mentioned: saying 'atlaqtuk' (I have released you) to a slave-girl is considered a kinayah for emancipation, and this ruling is attributed to Imam Abu Yusuf. However, saying 'I have divorced you' (tallaqtuk) is not effective for emancipation because the word 'divorce' is explicit in its own context and cannot be used for freeing a slave. The commentator promises further explanation of this contrast.

Key Terms
کنایات العتق (kinayat al-'itq): Metaphorical expressions that can imply emancipation but require intention because they share meanings with other legal actions.
أطلقتك (atlaqtuk): I have released you; a kinayah expression for emancipation, treated like 'khallaytu sabilak' according to Abu Yusuf.
خلیت سبیلک (khallaytu sabilak): I have set you free; a common kinayah expression for emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Quduri, which of the following is NOT a kinayah expression for emancipation?
A) You have come out of my ownership.
B) I have set you free.
C) I have divorced you.
D) I have released you.

Key Takeaway
For kinayah expressions of emancipation, intention is the decisive factor: without it, no emancipation occurs.

Ruling on Kinayah Words for Emancipation
Explanation of kinayah words requiring intention for emancipation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for kinayah words in emancipation and explain why intention is required.

Core Idea
Intention disambiguates ambiguity: kinayah requires niyyah for emancipation.

This passage from al-Quduri's text establishes that certain expressions (kinayah) are not explicit for emancipation; they could mean sale or emancipation. The Hanafi ruling clarifies that such words require the master's intention (niyyah) for emancipation to occur. Without intention, the slave remains enslaved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is using kinayah words like 'la mulka li 'alayka'. Ruling: emancipation occurs only if the master intended it.
The act itself is mubah; the legal effect is conditional on intention.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is talil (reasoning): kinayah words have multiple probabilities (sale or emancipation), so intention is needed to specify.
No direct textual proof from Quran or Sunnah; it's a rational inference.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase can mean either sale or emancipation. Since both are possible, the word is ambiguous (kinayah). To assign one meaning, the speaker's intention must be known.
This is the core of the Hanafi argument.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text contrasts kinayah with explicit words like 'tallaqtuk' (divorce) which are not effective for emancipation. Also mentions that Abu Yusuf's narration confirms the rule.
No intra-Hanafi disagreement; all three imams require intention.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that intention is necessary. Any kinayah word without intention does not free the slave.
This is the established fatwa in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مولی نے اپنے مملوک سے کہا لا ملک کی علیک اور اس کلام سے آزاد کرنے کی نیت کی تو یہ مملوک آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اگر آزادی کی نیت نہیں کی تو آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ کلام الفاظ کنایات میں سے ہے کیونکہ اس میں دو احتمال ہیں ایک یہ کہ میری ملک تجھ پر اسلئے نہیں کہ میں نے تجھے بیچ ڈالا اور دوسرا احتمال یہ ہے کہ ملک اس واسطے نہیں کہ میں نے تجھے آزاد کر دیا۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ یہ کلام آزاد کرنے کے معنی میں صریح نہیں ہے بلکہ کنائی ہے اور لفظ کنائی عمل میں نیت کا محتاج ہوتا ہے۔ اس کے بغیر نیت کے کوئی احتمال متعین نہیں ہو گا اور آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔
قال وكذا كنايات العتق وذلك مثل قوله خرجت من ملكي ولاسبيل لى علیک و لارق لی علیک وقد خلیت سبیلک لانه يحتمل نفى السبيل والخروج عن الملك و تخلية السبيل بالبيع او الكتابة كما يحتمل بالعتق فلابد من النية وكذا قوله لامته قد اطلقتك لانه بمنزلة قوله خليت سبیلک و هو المروی عن ابي يوسف بخلاف قوله طلقتك على مانبين من بعد ان شاء الله تعالى

Explanation: The scenario is such that if the master said to his slave 'la mulka li 'alayka' (no ownership upon you) and intended emancipation, then the slave becomes free. If he did not intend emancipation, emancipation does not occur. The proof is that this speech is among kinayah words because it has two probabilities: one is that my ownership is not upon you because I sold you, and the other is that ownership is not because I freed you. So it is established that this speech is not explicit in meaning emancipation but is kinayah, and a kinayah word requires intention to act upon. Without intention, no probability is determined and emancipation does not occur. He (al-Quduri) said: And likewise are the kinayah of emancipation, such as his saying 'you left my ownership, and there is no path for me upon you, and there is no slavery for me upon you, and I have vacated your way' because it can mean negation of path, leaving ownership, and vacating path by sale or kitabah, just as it can mean by emancipation. Thus intention is necessary. And likewise his saying to his female slave 'I have freed you' because it is equivalent to his saying 'I have vacated your way' and it is narrated from Abu Yusuf, unlike his saying 'I have divorced you' as we will explain later, God willing. Translation: al-Quduri said: The same applies to kinayah of emancipation, such as the master's saying 'you left my ownership, and there is no path for me upon you, and there is no slavery for me upon you, and I have vacated your way' because there is a possibility that vacating the path and leaving ownership is by sale or kitabah, similarly there is a possibility that it is by emancipation. Hence intention is necessary. And similarly if the master said to his female slave 'I have released you' because it is of the rank of 'khallaytu sabilak' and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Unlike if he said to her 'I have divorced you' as we will explain later, God willing. Explanation: Al-Quduri said that this applies to kinayah words of emancipation. That is, if one intended emancipation by kinayah words, then emancipation occurs, otherwise not. Examples: saying to his slave 'you left my ownership' or to his female slave 'you left my ownership', or 'there is no path for me upon you', or 'there is no slavery for me upon you', or 'I have vacated your way'. The proof is that all these words have a meaning other than emancipation. Because just as there is a meaning that I freed you, so you left my ownership or because I freed you my ownership is not upon you, etc., similarly, there is a probability that because I sold you or made you a mukatab, you left my ownership or my ownership is not upon you. So to determine one probability, intention is necessary. If intention is not found, emancipation does not occur. And similarly, if he said to his female slave 'I have released you', intention is also necessary because 'qad atlaqtuk' is of the rank of 'khallaytu sabilak'. This is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. Unlike if the master said to his female slave 'I have divorced you', in that case emancipation is not established despite intention, because this word is explicit in divorce, so emancipation is not established by it. We will explain this later, God willing.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling of kinayah (allusive) words in emancipation. The author begins by explaining the scenario: if a master says to his slave 'la mulka li 'alayka' (no ownership upon you) and intends emancipation, the slave becomes free. If he does not intend emancipation, it does not take effect. The reason is that this expression has two possible meanings: sale or emancipation. Since it is not explicit, it is kinayah, and kinayah requires intention. The passage then quotes al-Quduri's text, which states that all kinayah words for emancipation share the same ruling. Examples include 'kharafta min mulki' (you left my ownership), 'la sabila li 'alayka' (no path for me over you), 'la riqqa li 'alayka' (no slavery for me over you), and 'khallaytu sabilaka' (I vacated your way). These words can mean either emancipation or sale/kitabah, so intention is necessary. Similarly, saying to a female slave 'qad atlaqtuk' (I have freed you) is like 'khallaytu sabilak' and requires intention, as narrated from Abu Yusuf. However, if the master says 'tallaqtuk' (I divorce you), even with intention, emancipation does not occur because this word is explicit in divorce and cannot be used for emancipation. The passage emphasizes that the Hanafi position requires intention for all kinayah expressions, and this is based on the principle that ambiguous words must be disambiguated by niyyah. The text also highlights a distinction: while 'la sabila li 'alayka' is kinayah and requires intention, 'la sultana li 'alayka' has a different ruling, as discussed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الکنایة (al-kinayah): An ambiguous word that can mean either emancipation or something else (like sale) and requires intention to take effect.
النیة (al-niyyah): Intention to emancipate; necessary for kinayah words to produce the legal effect of emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave 'la mulka li 'alayka' without intending emancipation, what is the ruling?
A) The slave is emancipated because the words are clear.
B) The slave is not emancipated because intention is required for kinayah.
C) The slave is emancipated only if he is a minor.
D) The master must repeat the words with intention.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah words require intention of emancipation to be legally effective.

Master's Claim of Paternity and Emancipation
Ruling that a master's claim 'this is my son' leads to emancipation if conditions are met.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a master's claim of paternity ('hadha ibni') leads to emancipation of a slave, and explain the role of literal and metaphorical interpretation in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A master's persistent claim 'this is my son' frees the slave — even if literal paternity is impossible — by giving legal effect to his words metaphorically.

This passage from al-Hidayah examines a fascinating intersection of lineage, ownership, and the legal force of speech. When a master declares a slave his son, the Hanafi school treats this as a binding statement that triggers emancipation, subject to certain conditions. The reasoning relies on the master's authority over his property and the slave's need for lineage, deploying metaphorical interpretation (majaz) when literal meaning is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the master's statement 'hadha ibni' (this is my son) made to his slave. If the master persists in this claim, the ruling is that the slave becomes free (emancipation becomes wajib).
Persistence is key; a casual remark may not have the same effect.
2
Determine the condition of biological possibility
The ruling assumes that the slave's like can be born from the master's like (yuladu mithluhu li mithlihi). If this is impossible (e.g., master too young, age gap too large), a different ruling applies and is mentioned later in the text.
This condition ensures that the claim is not absurd from the outset.
3
Distinguish the two scenarios regarding lineage
The text presents two cases: (1) the slave has no known lineage – here the master's claim establishes the slave's lineage, and emancipation follows. (2) The slave has a known lineage – here literal paternity is impossible, but the slave still becomes free by giving the word 'ibni' a metaphorical meaning (e.g., 'my freed slave').
In both cases emancipation occurs, but via different reasoning.
4
Trace the legal reasoning (talil) for establishing lineage
When the slave has no known lineage, the master's ownership gives him the authority (wilaya) to claim paternity. The slave is in need of lineage (to avoid being of unknown origin), so the claim is accepted. Once lineage is established, it retroacts to the time of conception, meaning the slave was never a slave and becomes free.
This reasoning is unique to Hanafi fiqh and relies on the concept of wilayat al-da'wa.
5
Explain the use of majaz when literal meaning is impossible
When the slave has a known lineage, literal paternity is impossible (due to known father). To still give effect to the master's words, the term 'ibni' is interpreted metaphorically. The Hanafis adopt the principle that when a word cannot be applied literally, it is given its metaphorical meaning to uphold the legal act. Here 'ibni' means 'my freed slave' or 'my client'.
This demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to upholding the validity of speech acts whenever possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر مولیٰ نے اپنے مملوک سے کہا هذا ابنی اور اس پر قائم رہا مملوک آزاد ہو جائے گا
و لو قال هذا ابنى وثبت على ذلك عتق ومعنى المسألة اذا كان يولد مثله لمثله واذا كان لا يولد مثله لمثله ذكره بعد هذا ثم ان لم يكن للعبد نسب معروف ويثبت نسبه منه لان ولاية الدعوة بالملك ثابتة والعبد محتاج الى النسب فيثبت نسبه منه واذا ثبت عتق لانه يستند النسب الى وقت العلوق وان كان له نسب معروف لا يثبت نسه منه للتعذر ويعتق اعمالا للفظ فى مجازه عند تعذر اعماله بحقيقته ووجه المجازنذكره من بعد انشاء الله تعالى
ترجمہ اور اگر مولی نے (اپنے مملوک کو ) کہا کہ یہ میرا بیٹا ہے اور اس بات پر جما رہا تو آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اس مسئلہ کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ جب ایسا ہو کہ اس جیسا اس جیسے قائل سے پیدا ہو سکتا ہے اور اگر ایسا ہو کہ مولیٰ سے پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا۔ تو یہ مسئلہ اسکے بعد مذکور ہے۔ پھر اگر نام کا کوئی نسب معروف نہ ہو تو مولی سے اس کا نسب بھی ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ دعوی نسب کی ولایت مالک ہونے کی وجہ سے ثابت ہے اور غلام نسب کا محتاج بھی ہے۔ تو مولی سے اس کا نسب ثابت ہو جائے گا اور جب نسب ثابت ہوا تو آزاد ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ نسب،
If the master said to his slave 'this is my son' and persisted on that, the slave becomes free. And the meaning of the issue is if his (the slave's) like can be born from his (the master's) like, and if his like cannot be born from his like, it is mentioned later. Then if the slave has no known lineage, his lineage from him (the master) is established because the authority of claiming lineage is established by ownership, and the slave is in need of lineage, so his lineage from him is established. When lineage is established, he becomes free because lineage is attributed to the time of conception. And if he has a known lineage, his lineage from him is not established due to impossibility, but he becomes free to give effect to the word in its metaphorical sense when giving effect to its literal meaning is impossible. The explanation of the metaphor will be mentioned later, God willing. Translation: And if the master said (to his slave) 'this is my son' and persisted on that, he becomes free. The meaning of this issue is when a like can be born from a like. And if a like cannot be born from a like, it is mentioned later. Then if the slave has no known lineage, his lineage from the master is established because the authority of claiming lineage is established due to ownership, and the slave is in need of lineage. So his lineage from the master is established, and when lineage is established, he becomes free because lineage is attributed to the time of conception.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a master claims paternity of his slave by saying 'hadha ibni' (this is my son). If the master persists in this claim, the slave becomes free. However, certain conditions apply: the slave must be of an age where the master could biologically be his father (yuladu mithluhu li mithlihi). If that is not possible (e.g., the master is too young), the ruling is different and mentioned later. Additionally, if the slave has no known lineage, his lineage becomes established from the master because the master has the authority to claim paternity over his property. Since the slave needs lineage, it is established. Once lineage is established, the slave becomes free because the lineage traces back to the time of conception, implying that he was never a slave. If the slave already has a known lineage, his lineage from the master cannot be established (to avoid confusion), but he still becomes free by giving effect to the master's words metaphorically: the meaning of 'ibni' is taken as 'my freed slave' or 'my client' rather than literal son. The Hanafi position uses this metaphorical interpretation to uphold the master's statement and effect emancipation even when literal paternity is impossible. The text notes that the detailed explanation of the metaphor will follow later (an allusion to further discussion in the Hidayah).

Key Terms
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage; in this context, establishing paternity from the master.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning; when literal meaning is impossible, the word is interpreted metaphorically to effect emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave's consent.
B) The master's ownership gives him authority to claim paternity.
C) The testimony of two witnesses.
D) A court order.

Key Takeaway
A master's persistent claim 'hadha ibni' obligates emancipation of the slave, using literal lineage establishment when possible and metaphorical interpretation when necessary.

The Ruling on 'La Sultana Li Alayka' for Emancipation
Explains legal effect of master's statements denying authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between sultan (possession) and sabil (ownership) to determine whether a master's statement effects emancipation.

Core Idea
Negating authority (sultan) does not free a slave because authority is possession, not ownership — unlike negating 'way' (sabil).

This passage from the Hidayah commentary examines the precise legal effect of a master's words on a slave. The Hanafi position relies on the linguistic distinction between 'sultan' (authority/possession) and 'sabil' (path/ownership). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of implied emancipation in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two statements and their rulings
The master says (1) 'la sultana li alayka' – no authority over you; (2) 'la sabila li alayka' – no way over you. For the first, with intent, the slave is not freed; for the second, he is freed.
Focus on the different outcomes despite similar wording.
2
Understand the definition of 'sultan' in this context
'Sultan' refers to possession (qabd), not ownership (milk). The king is called sultan because he has possession over his kingdom.
This is a key linguistic point.
3
Recognize that ownership can exist without possession
The case of the mukatib (slave under contract of manumission) shows that a master still owns the slave but does not have full possession.
This analogy (qiyas) proves that negating possession does not negate ownership.
4
Contrast with 'la sabila li alayka'
'Sabil' implies a path of ownership. Negating it absolutely negates the master's right/ownership, so when coupled with intention, it effects emancipation.
The master still has a 'sabil' over a mukatib, so the negation of 'sabil' is stronger.
5
Derive the Hanafi ruling and note the epistemological weight
The ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah is that no emancipation occurs with 'la sultana'. The proof is qiyas to the mukatib case.
This is a settled Hanafi position with later endorsement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد پنجم
مولی نے کہالا سلطان لی علیک اس سے آزاد کرنے کی نیت کی آزاد نہیں ہوگا
ولوقال لاسلطان لی علیک ونوى العتق لم يعتق لان السلطان عبارة عن اليد وسمى السلطان به لقيام يده و قد يبقى الملك دون اليد كما فى المكاتب بخلاف قوله لا سبيل لى عليك لان نفيه مطلقاً بانتفاء الملك لان للمولى على المكاتب سبيلا فلهذا يحتمل العتق
ترجمہ اور اگر (مولی نے ) کہا تجھ پر میری سلطنت نہیں ہے اور آزادی کی نیت کی تو آزاد نہ ہوگا ۔ اسلئے کہ سلطنت سے مراد قبضہ ہے اور نام رکھا گیا بادشاہ کا سلطان۔ اسلئے کہ (مملکت پر ) اس کا قبضہ ہے اور کبھی ملک باقی رہتی ہے نہ کہ قبضہ جیسے مکاتب میں ہوتا ہے۔ بر خلاف اس کے قول لا سبیل لی علیک کے۔ کیونکہ مطابق راہ کی نفی کر نا ملک کی نفی سے ہوتا ہے کیونکہ مکاتب پر مولی کی راہ باقی رہتی ہے۔ اسلئے یہ آزادی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مالک نے اپنے مملوک سے کہا لا سلطان کی علیک اور آزادی کی نیت کی تو مملوک آزاد نہ ہوگا ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ سلطان سے مراد قبضہ ہے اور بادشاہ کو بھی سلطان اس لئے کہتے ہیں کہ اس کا مملکت پر قبضہ ہوتا ہے۔ جس طرح چاہے تصرف کرے اور ملک اور قبضہ کے درمیان تلازم نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ ملکیت باقی رہتی ہے اور قبضہ نہیں رہتا جیسے مکاتب میں ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر مولی نے اپنے مملوک سے کہا لا سبیل لی علیک اور آزادی کی نیت کی تو مملوک آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ بالکل سبیل ( راہ) کی نفی کرنا جب ہی ہو سکتا ہے جبکہ ملک نہ ہو اور چونکہ مکاتب پر مولی کی راہ باقی رہتی ہے۔ اس لیے لا سبيل لـ علیک آزادی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔
فوائد مصنف کی عبارت السلطان عبارة عن الید میں تسامح ہے ۔ کیونکہ سلطان قبضہ کو نہیں کہتے ۔ بلکہ صاحب قبضہ کو کہتے ہے۔
And if [the master] said: 'I have no authority over you' and intended emancipation, he is not emancipated, because 'sultan' is an expression for possession, and the king is called sultan because of his possession. And ownership may remain without possession, as in the case of the mukatib. Unlike his saying 'I have no way over you', because its negation absolutely occurs by negation of ownership, for the master has a way over the mukatib, so this bears the possibility of emancipation.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) regarding the effect of a master's words 'la sultana li alayka' on the slave's freedom. The commentator explains that 'sultan' refers to possession (qabd), not ownership (milk). The king is called sultan because he has possession over his kingdom. Ownership can exist without possession, as in the case of a mukatib (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom). Therefore, if the master says 'no authority over you', he is only negating possession, not ownership, so the slave does not become free even if intended. The contrast is with 'la sabila li alayka' (no way over you), where 'sabil' implies a path of ownership; negating it absolutely denies ownership, so if intention is present, the slave is freed. The author also notes a subtle correction: calling sultan 'possession' is not precise; rather, sultan is the one who possesses, not the possession itself.

Key Terms
سلطان (sultan): In this context, it means possession (qabd) or authority through possession.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has made a contract with his master to purchase his freedom, still owned but with limited master's rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave becomes free because authority is denied.
B) The slave does not become free because sultan refers to possession, not ownership.
C) The slave becomes free only if the intention is expressed verbally.
D) The slave becomes free only if he is a mukatib.

Key Takeaway
Sultan in this context means possession, not ownership; therefore, negating sultan does not negate the master's ownership, and the slave remains unfreed.
