Metaphorical Interpretation: Abu Hanifa's Ruling on 'Hadha Ibni'
Dispute on metaphorical interpretation of 'hadha ibni'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's principle of metaphorical interpretation (majaz) to determine the legal effect of a literally impossible statement and distinguish it from cases where no metaphor is possible.

Core Idea
A literally impossible statement is not automatically null; if a metaphorical meaning is possible, it is given effect to avoid لغو.

This passage plunges into a classic Hanafi dispute: when a master says 'this is my son' to a slave who obviously cannot be his biological child, does the slave go free? The Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i say no—the statement is impossible, so it's meaningless. But Abu Hanifa argues that we must save the speaker's words from nullity by interpreting them metaphorically. Understanding this reasoning reveals how Hanafi usul uses linguistic principles (majaz, sabab, lazim) to derive practical fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the core fact pattern
Identify that the master says 'hadha ibni' to a slave who cannot be his biological son (e.g., due to age). Recognize that the literal meaning is impossible.
2
Identify the opposing positions
Note that Abu Hanifa rules the slave is freed, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i rule it is not freed.
The dispute is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the opponents' proof
The opponents analogize to 'I freed you before creation' — a literally impossible statement that is null. They argue the same should apply here.
The key is the lack of any metaphorical path in the 'before creation' analogy.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes: here there is a valid metaphorical connection — sonship is a cause for freedom (or freedom is a necessary attribute of sonship). Thus, the statement is interpreted metaphorically as an announcement of freedom from the time of ownership.
Two metaphorical paths are given: 1) mentioning cause intending effect, 2) likening to a necessary attribute.
5
Evaluate the distinctions
Contrast with the 'hand-cutting' analogy: freedom is uniform in essence and ruling, so metaphor works; financial liability from cutting is specific (arsh, due on 'aqila) and cannot be established without the act.
This shows the limits of metaphorical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان قال لغلام لا يولد مثله لمثله هذا ابنى عتق عند ابي حنيفة وقالا لا يعتق وهو قول الشافعى لهم انه كلام محال بحقيقته فيرد ويلغو كقوله اعتقتك قبل ان اخلق او قبل ان تخلق ولابي حنيفة انه كلام محال بحقيقته لكنه صحيح بمجازه لانه اخبار عن حريته من حين ملكه وهذا لان البنوة في المملوك سبب لحريته اما اجماعا اوصلة للقرابة واطلاق السبب و ارادة المسبب مستجاز فى اللغة تجوز اولان الحرية لازمة للبنوة في المملوك والمشابهة في وصف لازم من طرق المجاز على ماعرف فيحمل عليه تحرزا عن الالغاء بخلاف ما استشهد به لانه لا وجه له فى المجاز فتعين الالغاء وهذا بخلاف ما اذا قال لغيره قطعت یدک فاخرجهما صحيحتين حيث لم يجعل مجازا عن الاقرار بالمال والتزامه وان كان القطع سبب لوجوب المال لان القطع خطأ سبب لوجوب مال مخصوص و هو الارش وانه يخالف مطلق المال في الوصف حتى وجب على العاقلة في سنتين ولا يمكن اثباته بدون القطع وما امكن اثباته فالقطع ليس بسبب اما الحرية لاتختلف ذاتا و حكما فامكن جعله مجازاعنه
له
ترجمہ اور اگر ایسے غلام کو جس کے مثل اس سے پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے کہا کہ یہ میرا بیٹا ہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک آزاد ہو جائے گا اور صاحبین نے کہا کہ آزاد نہیں ہوگا اور یہی امام شافعی کا قول ہے ( اور ) ان فقہاء کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ کلام اپنے حقیقی معنی کے ساتھ محال ہے تو مردود اور لغو ہو جائے گا۔ جیسے اس کا قول کہ میں نے تجھے اپنے پیدا ہونے سے پہلے یا تیرے پیدا ہونے سے پہلے آزاد کر دیا تھا اور ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ کلام اپنے حقیقی معنی کے اعتبار سے (اگر چہ) محال ہے۔ لیکن مجازی معنی کے اعتبار سے صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ اس کے مالک ہونے کے وقت سے اس کے آزاد ہونے کی خبر دینا ہے اور یہ اسلئے کہ مملوک کا بیٹا ہونا اس کی آزادی کا سبب ہے ۔ یا اجماع کی وجہ سے یا صلہ قرابت کی وجہ سے اور سبب بول کر مسبب مراد لینا لغت میں مجاز ہے اور اسلئے کہ مملوک میں بیٹا ہونے کیلئے آزادی لازم ہے اور وصف لازم میں تشبیہ دینا، مجاز کے طریقوں میں سے ایک طریقہ ) ہے۔ جیسا کہ (اصول میں ) معلوم ہو چکا۔ تو کلام کولغو ہونے سے بچانے کیلئے مجازی معنی پر محمول کیا جائے گا۔ بخلاف اس مسئلہ کہ جس سے صاحبین اور امام شافعی نے شہادت پیش کی ہے۔ کیونکہ اسمیں کوئی طریقہ مجاز کا نہیں ہے تو اس کا لغو ہو نا متعین ہو گیا اور یہ بخلاف اس کے کہ ایک شخص نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ میں نے ( غلطی ) سے تیرا ہاتھ کاٹ دیا۔ بس اس نے اپنے دونوں ہاتھ تندرست نکال کر دکھلا دیئے تو اس قول کو اقرار بالمال اور التزام مال سے مجاز قرار نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ اگر چہ خطاء ہاتھ کا شامال مخصوص یعنی ارش ( جرمانہ ) واجب ہونے کا سبب ہے اور یہ مطلق مال کے ساتھ ایک وصف میں مخالف ہے۔
And if he said to a slave whose likeness cannot be born from his likeness, 'This is my son', it is freed according to Abu Hanifa, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he is not freed, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i. Their proof is that this speech is impossible in its literal meaning, so it is rejected and become لغو (null), like his saying 'I freed you before I was created' or 'before you were created'. According to Abu Hanifa, this speech is impossible in its literal meaning, but it is correct in its metaphorical meaning, because it is an announcement of his freedom from the time of his ownership. This is because sonship in a slave is a cause for his freedom, either by consensus or by connection of kinship. And mentioning the cause while intending the effect is permissible as metaphor in language. Also, because freedom is necessary for sonship in a slave, and likening something to a necessary attribute is one of the ways of metaphor, as is known. So it is interpreted metaphorically to avoid nullity. Contrary to what they cited as evidence, because there is no way for metaphor there, so nullity is determined. And this is contrary to if someone said to another 'I cut off your hand' and the latter showed both hands healthy, where it is not considered as a metaphor for admission of financial liability, even though cutting is a cause for obligation of wealth, because cutting by mistake is a cause for a specific wealth, which is the diyat (blood money), and it differs from absolute wealth in description, so it is obligatory on the 'aqila in two years, and it cannot be proven without the cutting. What can be proven? The cutting is not a cause. But freedom does not differ in essence and ruling, so it is possible to make it a metaphor for it. Translation: And if he said to a slave of whom the likes cannot be born from his likes 'This is my son', then according to Imam Abu Hanifa he becomes free, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he is not free, and this is the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i. Their proof is that this speech is impossible in its literal meaning, so it is rejected and null, like his saying 'I freed you before I was created' or 'before you were created'. According to Abu Hanifa, this speech is impossible in its literal meaning, but it is correct in its metaphorical meaning, because it is an announcement of his freedom from the time of his ownership. This is because sonship in a slave is a cause for his freedom, either by consensus or by connection of kinship. And mentioning the cause while intending the effect is permissible in language as metaphor. Also, because freedom is necessary for sonship in a slave, and likening with a necessary attribute is one of the ways of metaphor, as is known in usul. So the speech is interpreted metaphorically to avoid nullity. Contrary to the case they cited as evidence, because there is no way for metaphor there, so nullity is determined. And this is contrary to if a person said to another 'I cut off your hand' and the other showed both hands healthy, where this statement is not made metaphor for admission of financial liability or commitment, even though cutting is a cause for obligation of wealth, because cutting by mistake is a cause for a specific wealth, which is the 'arsh (indemnity), and it differs from absolute wealth in description, to the extent that it is obligatory on the 'aqila in two years, and it cannot be proven without the cutting, and what can be proven? The cutting is not a cause. But freedom does not differ in essence and ruling, so it is possible to make it a metaphor for it.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (sharh) on an inter-madhhab dispute. The issue: if a master says of a slave who obviously cannot be his biological son (e.g., due to age difference) 'hadha ibni' (this is my son), does the slave become free? Abu Hanifa holds that he becomes free, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that he does not. The opponents argue that the statement is literally impossible, so it is null and void, like saying 'I freed you before I existed'. Abu Hanifa responds that although the literal meaning is impossible, the statement can be interpreted metaphorically. He provides two reasons: 1) Sonship in a slave is a cause for freedom (either by consensus or because kinship implies freedom in this context); mentioning the cause while intending the effect is a recognized metaphor in Arabic. 2) Freedom is a necessary attribute of sonship in a slave (since a slave who is acknowledged as a son becomes free); comparing something to its necessary attribute is also a valid form of metaphor. To avoid nullifying the speaker's words, we interpret them metaphorically as a declaration of freedom from the time of ownership. Abu Hanifa then distinguishes this from two analogies used by the opponents: a) the case of 'I freed you before creation' (no metaphorical path exists, so null), and b) the case of someone saying 'I cut off your hand' when the hand is healthy (this cannot be metaphor for financial liability because the obligation differs in nature—it is a specific indemnity due on the 'aqila, not absolute wealth). Freedom, however, is uniform in essence and ruling, so it can be metaphorically intended.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning; using a word in other than its literal sense due to a connection.
سبب (sabab): Cause; here, sonship is a cause for freedom in a slave.
لغو (laghw): Null, meaningless speech that has no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the statement 'hadha ibni' to a slave who cannot be his son valid?
A) Because the literal meaning is still possible.
B) Because the statement can be interpreted metaphorically as an announcement of freedom.
C) Because the slave confessed.
D) Because it is an admission of paternity.

Key Takeaway
When a statement is literally impossible but a valid metaphorical interpretation exists, Hanafi fiqh adopts the metaphorical meaning to avoid nullity.

Impossible Paternity: Does Metaphor Free the Slave?
Inter-madhhab dispute on metaphorical paternity claim freeing a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of majāz to resolve a case of impossible literal speech and explain why the slave is freed according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is impossible, speech can be saved by metaphorical interpretation to avoid nullification.

This lesson examines a fascinating dispute: a master says 'this is my son' about a slave who cannot possibly be his biological son. Does the slave become free? Abu Hanifa says yes, through metaphorical interpretation. The Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i say no, the statement is null. We will explore the reasoning behind each position and understand the Hanafi commitment to preserving the speaker's intent via majāz.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual impossibility
The slave is of a type such that a person like the master cannot have a son like the slave (e.g., different race or age). Explain why literal paternity is impossible.
This is the trigger for the dispute.
2
State the dispute and positions
Identify the differing rulings: Abu Hanifa frees the slave; Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i do not.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf plus cross-madhhab disagreement.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
The Sahibayn argue that impossible literal speech is null (laghw), analogizing to 'I freed you before creation.'
They insist on literal possibility for speech to be effective.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof via majāz
Two pathways: (a) using the cause (sonship) to mean the effect (freedom), and (b) using a necessary attribute (freedom is necessary for sonship in a slave) as metaphor. The key is to avoid nullification.
Hanafis prioritize avoiding ilghā'.
5
Distinguish the counterexample
Explain why the hand-cutting case is different: the specific blood-money cannot be proven without actual cutting, so no metaphor is possible.
This reinforces the limits of majāz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان قال لغلام لا يولد مثله لمثله هذا ابنى عتق عند ابي حنيفة وقالا لا يعتق وهو قول الشافعى لهم انه كلام محال بحقيقته فيرد ويلغو كقوله اعتقتك قبل ان اخلق او قبل ان تخلق ولابي حنيفة انه كلام محال بحقيقته لكنه صحيح بمجازه لانه اخبار عن حريته من حين ملكه وهذا لان البنوة في المملوك سبب لحريته اما اجماعا او صلة للقرابة واطلاق السبب و ارادة المسبب مستجاز فى اللغة تجوز اولان الحرية لازمة للبنوة في المملوك والمشابهة في وصف لازم من طرق المجاز على ماعرف فيحمل عليه تحرزا عن الالغاء بخلاف ما استشهد به لانه لا وجه له فى المجاز فتعين الالغاء وهذا بخلاف ما اذا قال لغيره قطعت یدک فاخرجهما صحيحتين حيث لم يجعل مجازا عن الاقرار بالمال والتزامه وان كان القطع سبب لوجوب المال لان القطع خطأ سبب لوجوب مال مخصوص و هو الارش وانه يخالف مطلق المال في الوصف حتى وجب على العاقلة في سنتين ولا يمكن اثباته بدون القطع وما امكن اثباته فالقطع ليس بسبب اما الحرية لاتختلف ذاتا و حكما فامكن جعله مجازاعنه
And if he says to a slave of whom a person like him cannot be born from someone like him: 'This is my son,' then according to Abu Hanifa he is freed, and the two said he is not freed, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i. Their proof: it is speech impossible in its literal meaning, so it is rejected and considered null, like his saying 'I freed you before I was created or before you were created.' And for Abu Hanifa: it is speech impossible in its literal meaning, but it is valid by its metaphorical meaning because it is informing about his freedom from the time he owned him. And this is because Sonship in a slave is a cause for his freedom, either by consensus or as a connection of kinship. And using the cause while intending the effect is permissible in language as a metaphor. Also, freedom is necessary for sonship in a slave, and likening in a necessary attribute is one of the ways of metaphor, as known. So it is interpreted accordingly to avoid nullification, contrary to what was cited as evidence, because there is no way for metaphor in it, so nullification is determined. And this is contrary to the case where he says to another: 'I cut off your hand,' and he brings them out healthy, where it is not made a metaphor for acknowledgment of wealth and its obligation, even though cutting is a cause for the obligation of wealth. Because cutting by mistake is a cause for the obligation of specific wealth, which is the blood-money, and it differs from absolute wealth in its quality, such that it is obligatory on the 'āqila in two years, and it cannot be proven without the cutting. And what can be proven, cutting is not a cause for it. As for freedom, it does not differ in essence and legal effect, so it is possible to make it a metaphor for it.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning a master's paternity claim over a slave who could not possibly be his son. The ruling: According to Abu Hanifa, the slave is freed; according to Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i, he is not freed. The Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i argue that the speech is impossible in its literal sense, so it must be rejected as null, similar to saying 'I freed you before creation.' Abu Hanifa concedes the literal impossibility but argues that the speech can be valid metaphorically: the master intends to inform of the slave's freedom from the moment of ownership. Sonship (banuwwa) in a slave is a cause for freedom, either by consensus (ijma') or by connection of kinship (sila li-l-qaraba). Using the cause while meaning the effect is a recognized majāz (metaphor). Alternatively, freedom is a necessary attribute (lāzim) of sonship in a slave, and describing something by its necessary attribute is also a form of majāz. To avoid nullifying the speech, it should be interpreted metaphorically. This is unlike the court case where a person claims to have cut off another's hand but the hand is intact—such speech is not taken as an acknowledgment of debt because the cause (cutting) is specific and cannot be proven without it, and freedom does not differ in essence or ruling, so metaphor is possible.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): Here it means using a word in a non-literal sense based on a relationship (like cause-effect or inherent attribute) when the literal meaning is impossible.
سبب (sabab): A cause that leads to a legal effect; here, sonship is considered a cause (sabab) for freedom.
الغاء (ilghā'): Nullification or rendering inoperative. The principle is to avoid ilghā' of speech if a valid interpretation (majāz) exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best explains why Abu Hanifa frees the slave in the case of an impossible paternity claim?
A) Because the master's statement must be taken literally even if impossible.
B) Because the speech is interpreted via majāz, using the cause-effect relationship between sonship and freedom.
C) Because the slave's consent is presumed.
D) Because the Sahibayn also agree with Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
When literal meaning is impossible, the Hanafi school applies majāz to avoid nullification, freeing the slave.

Master Saying 'This is My Son' to an Older Slave
Intra-madhhab dispute on emancipation by declaration of paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute regarding emancipation by declaration of paternity and apply the concepts of majaz, sabab, and malzum wa lazim to distinguish between valid and invalid figurative interpretations.

Core Idea
A master's literal impossibility may still free a slave if the statement carries a valid figurative connection.

This passage examines a disagreement among the Hanafi imams about whether a master's impossible declaration 'this is my son' to an older slave can nonetheless free the slave. Abu Hanifa argues that the statement is a valid metaphor because sonship necessarily implies freedom, while the Sahibayn contend that impossible literal speech is futile. Understanding this dispute requires grappling with the Hanafi theory of majaz (figurative interpretation) and the relationship between cause and effect (sabab-musabbab) or the necessitated and necessary concomitant (malzum wa lazim).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
A master says 'this is my son' to a slave older than him. The dispute is whether this frees the slave.
2
State each side's position
Imam Abu Hanifa says the slave is freed; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he is not freed.
The Shafi'i school agrees with the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the evidence
Abu Hanifa uses figurative interpretation (majaz) – sonship is a cause (sabab) that necessitates freedom, so mentioning cause intends effect. The Sahibayn argue that when literal meaning is impossible, the speech is futile and cannot be given a figurative meaning.
The Sahibayn analogize to 'I freed you before creation' as an example of futile speech.
4
Evaluate the objections and responses
The Sahibayn raise the hand-cutting objection: saying 'I cut your hand' without the hand being cut is not interpreted figuratively. Abu Hanifa distinguishes because arsh (compensation) is a specific type of money that depends on the hand being cut, whereas freedom is a uniform ruling without such dependence.
The key is the nature of the necessary connection: in the slave case, sonship intrinsically implies freedom; in the hand case, the connection is not direct.

Source Text

تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مولی نے اپنے غلام کو کہا ھذا ابنى درانحالیکہ وہ غلام عمر میں اپنے مولی سے بڑا ہے۔ یعنی اس جیسے غلام کا اس جیسے مولی سے پیدا ہونا ناممکن ہے تو اس صورت میں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا اور صاحبین اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ آزاد نہیں ہو گا۔ صاحبین اور امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس کلام کا حقیقی معنی پرمحمول کرنا محال ہے۔ لہذا یہ کلام مرد و داور لغو ہو گا۔ جیسے یہ کہنا کہ میں نے تجھے اپنے پیدا ہونے سے پہلے یا تیرے پیدا ہونے سے پہلے آزاد کر دیا تھا تو یہ کلام لغو ہے۔ اسی طرح اپنے سے زیادہ عمر والے کو هذا ابنی کہنا بھی لغو ہو گا۔ رہا یہ اعتراض کہ اور اس کلام کو معنی حقیقی یعنی بنوت پر محمول کرنا محال ہے۔ تو معنی مجازی یعنی حریت پر محمول کیا جائے اور ظاہر ہے کہ اس صورت میں بھی غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اگر معنی حقیقی پر عمل کرنا محال ہو تو معنی مجازی بھی ثابت نہیں ہوں گے۔ اسلئے کہ مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ ہوتا ہے۔ پس جب اصل یعنی حقیقت پر حکم لگانا ممکن نہیں ہے تو خلیفہ پر بھی حکم لگا نا ممکن نہیں ہوگا اور جب حقیقت اور مجاز دونوں ممکن الحکم نہیں ہیں تو یہ کلام لغو ہو گا اور ظاہر ہے کہ کلام لغو ہونے کی صورت میں آزادی واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔
امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ کلام اگر چہ اپنے حقیقی معنی ( بنوت) کے اعتبار سے محال ہے لیکن مجازی معنی (حریت ) کے اعتبار سے صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ مولیٰ نے اس بات کی خبر دی ہے کہ جب سے میں اس غلام کا مالک ہوا ہوں ۔ یہ آزاد ہے اور یہ اس وجہ سے ہے کہ مملوک کا بیٹا ہونا اس کی آزادی کا سبب ہے۔ یعنی مملوک کے واسطے بیٹا ہونے کا ثبوت اسی وقت ہو سکتا ہے جبکہ وہ آزاد ہو۔ حاصل یہ کہ بیٹا ہونا آزادی کا سبب ہے یا تو دلیل اجتماع کی وجہ سے یا صلہ قرابت کی وجہ سے۔ اسلئے کہ بنوت صلہ رحمی کو واجب کرتا ہے اور آزادی صلہ ہے۔ پس بنوت ( بیٹا ہونا ) آزادی کو واجب کرتا ہے۔ پس یہاں سبب یعنی بنوت بول کر مسبب یعنی آزادی مراد لی گئی ہے اور سبب بول کر مسبب مراد لینا مجاز ہے۔ اس وجہ سے یہ کلام اپنے مجازی معنی کے اعتبار سے صحیح ہے۔
دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مملوک میں بیٹا ہونے کے واسطے آزادی لازم ہے کیونکہ بیٹا اپنے باپ کا مملوک نہیں ہو سکتا۔ پس بیٹا ہو نا ملزوم اور آزادی اس کا لازم ہوا اور وصف لازم کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا بھی ایک قسم کا مجاز ہے۔ لہذا کلام کو لغو ہونے سے بچانے کیلئے معنی مجازی پر محمول کیا جائے گا۔ یعنی ملزوم یعنی هذا ابنی سے اس کا لازم یعنی هذاخر مراد لیا جائے گا۔ اسکے برخلاف وہ مسئلہ جس کو صاحبین اور امام شافعی نے شہادت میں پیش کیا ہے۔ یعنی اعتقتک قبل ان اخلق يا قبل ان تخلق اس کو شہادت میں پیش کرنا درست نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ پیدا ہونے سے پہلے آزادی ممکن نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے اس کلام میں مجاز کی کوئی وجہ موجود نہیں ہے۔ لہذا یہ کلام لغو ہو گا اور قابل استشہاد نہیں ہوگا۔
و هذا بخلاف ما اذا قال لغیرہ سے اشکال کا جواب ہے۔ اشکال یہ ہے کہ امام صاحب نے فرمایا کہ ملزوم بول کر لازم مراد لینا مجاز ہے اور کلام اگر حقیقی معنی کے اعتبار سے محال ہو تو مجازی معنی پر محمول کیا جائے گا۔ مگر ہم دیکھتے ہیں کہ امام صاحب نے ایک مسئلہ میں اس اصول پر عمل نہیں کیا ہے۔ مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ میں نے دھوکے سے تیرا ہاتھ کاٹ دیا ہے۔ پس اس نے اپنے دونوں ہاتھ صحیح سالم نکال کر دکھلا دئیے۔ اس کا یہ کلام مجاز پر محمول نہیں کیا جاتا ہے۔ یعنی یہ نہیں کہا جاتا کہ اس نے ایک ہاتھ کے تاوان کی مقدارا اپنے او پر مال لازم کیا ہے۔ حالانکہ خطا ہاتھ کا شامال واجب ہونے کا سبب ہے۔ پس سبب یعنی قطعت یدک بول کر مسبب یعنی وجوب مال مراد لینا چاہئے تھا۔ جیسا کہ هذا ابنى بول کر هذا حر مراد لیا گیا ہے۔
جواب یہ ہے کہ خطاء ہاتھ کاٹنا ایک خاص قسم کے مال یعنی ارش (جرمانہ ) واجب ہونے کا سبب ہوتا ہے نہ کہ مطلق مال واجب ہونے کا سبب اور یہ مال مخصوص (ارش) ایک وصف میں مطلق مال کے مخالف ہے۔ چنانچہ یہ مال مخصوص یعنی ارش عاقله ( مددگار برادری ) پر دو سال میں واجب ہوتا ہے اور مطلق مال میں یہ بات نہیں ہے اور یہ مال مخصوص جو عاقلہ پر واجب ہوتا ہے۔ اس کا ثابت کرنا بغیر ہاتھ کائے ممکن نہیں ہے اور مال مطلق یعنی اقرار قرضہ جس کا ثابت کرنا ممکن ہے مگر ہاتھ کا بنا اس کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ اس صورت میں حقیقت اور مجاز دونوں متعذر ہیں۔ حقیقت کا متعدد ہونا تو ظاہر ہے اسلئے کہ اس کا ہاتھ کٹا ہوا نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ تندرست اور صحیح ہے اور مجاز اسلئے متعذر ہے کہ خطا سے ہاتھ کا نامز دوم اور ارش اس کا لازم ہے اور ارش ملزوم اور ہاتھ کاٹنا اس کا لازم ہے اور لازم یعنی ہاتھ کا شنا باطل ہے۔ لہذا لزوم یعنی ارش ( جرمانہ ) بھی باطل ہوگا اور رہی غلام کے مسئلہ میں آزادی تو وہ ذات و حکم میں مختلف نہیں ہے۔ لہذا بیٹا کہہ کر مجاز ا آزادی مراد لینا ممکن ہے۔
Explanation of the issue: If a master said to his slave 'This is my son' while the slave is older than the master, i.e., it is impossible for such a slave to be born from such a master, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa this slave becomes free, but al-Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i said he does not become free. The evidence of al-Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i is that applying the literal meaning of this speech is impossible, so this speech is void and futile, like saying 'I freed you before I was created' or 'before you were created' — such speech is futile. Similarly, calling someone older 'this is my son' is also futile. As for the objection that since applying the literal meaning (sonship) is impossible, it should be applied figuratively (freedom), and obviously in that case the slave would be free. The answer is: If applying the literal meaning is impossible, then the figurative meaning is also not established, because the figurative is the deputy of the literal. So when the original (literal) cannot be ruled upon, the deputy also cannot be ruled upon. And when both literal and figurative cannot be ruled upon, this speech is futile, and clearly when speech is futile, freedom does not occur. Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence: This speech, although impossible in its literal meaning (sonship), is valid in its figurative meaning (freedom), because the master informed that since he owned this slave, he is free. This is because a slave's being a son implies his freedom, i.e., proof of sonship for a slave can only be when he is free. In short, sonship is a cause of freedom, either due to combining evidence or due to maintenance of kinship, because sonship necessitates maintenance, and freedom is a gift. So sonship necessitates freedom. Here the cause (sonship) is mentioned but the effect (freedom) is intended, and mentioning cause for effect is a metaphor. Therefore this speech is valid in its figurative meaning. Second evidence: For a slave, sonship necessitates freedom because a son cannot be owned by his father. So sonship is the necessitated thing and freedom is its necessary concomitant, and likening to a necessary attribute is also a kind of metaphor. To save the speech from futility, it is applied figuratively, i.e., from the necessitated (this is my son) its necessary concomitant (this is free) is intended. Contrary to the case that al-Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i presented as a witness: 'I freed you before I was created' or 'before you were created' — this is not correct to present as evidence, because freedom before creation is impossible, and in that speech there is no basis for metaphor, so it is futile and cannot be used as evidence. And 'this is different from when he said to someone else' is the answer to the objection. The objection: Imam Abu Hanifa said that mentioning the necessitated and intending the necessary concomitant is metaphor, and if speech is impossible literally, it will be applied figuratively. But we see that Imam Abu Hanifa did not apply this principle in a case: A person said to another 'I cut off your hand by mistake' and then showed both his hands intact. This speech is not applied figuratively, i.e., it is not said that he obligated upon himself the compensation for a hand, even though mistakenly cutting a hand is a cause for compensation. So he should have mentioned the cause 'I cut your hand' intending the effect 'obligation of money', just as 'this is my son' is intended to mean 'this is free'. The answer: Mistakenly cutting a hand is a cause for a specific type of money, i.e., arsh (blood money), not for absolute money. And this specific money (arsh) is opposite to absolute money in one attribute. Arsh is obligatory upon the 'aqila (tribal helpers) over two years, while absolute money is not. And this specific money obligatory on the 'aqila cannot be proven without cutting the hand, while absolute money (like acknowledging a debt) can be proven without that. So in this case both literal and figurative are impossible. Literal impossibility is obvious because his hand is not cut but healthy; figurative impossibility because mistakenly cutting a hand requires the hand to be cut (the necessitated) and arsh is its necessary concomitant, but the necessary concomitant (the cut hand) is false, so the necessitator (arsh) is also false. As for freedom in the slave case, it does not differ in essence and ruling, so calling him 'son' and figuratively intending freedom is possible.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the effect of a master calling an older slave 'my son'. The core issue is whether such a statement, impossible literally, can be interpreted figuratively to result in emancipation. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Imam al-Shafi'i argue that when literal meaning is impossible, the speech becomes futile and cannot be applied figuratively because metaphor depends on the literal meaning as its foundation. Imam Abu Hanifa counters that the relationship between sonship and freedom is such that sonship necessitates freedom, so mentioning 'son' is a metonymy for 'free'. He provides two dala'il: (1) sonship is a cause for freedom (by reason of maintenance or kinship), so cause is mentioned for effect; (2) sonship is the necessitated thing (malzum) and freedom is its necessary concomitant (lazim), so mentioning the malzum and intending the lazim is a valid metaphor analogized to describing something by its essential attribute. The text then addresses an objection based on a case where someone says 'I cut your hand by mistake' but both hands are intact; here the principle is not applied because the specific type of money (arsh) is different in nature from absolute money, and its proof depends on the hand being cut, so both literal and figurative are impossible. The key point: In the slave case, freedom is a uniform ruling that can be proven without a physical precondition, whereas arsh requires the hand's existence.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Figurative or metaphorical usage of a word, where the literal meaning is impossible and a related meaning is intended.
سبب (sabab): Cause; here, sonship is considered the cause (sabab) for freedom, so mentioning the cause indicates the effect.
ملزوم و لازم (malzum wa lazim): The necessitated thing (malzum) and its necessary concomitant (lazim); sonship is malzum, freedom is lazim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why does a master's statement 'this is my son' to an older slave result in the slave's freedom?
A) Because the statement is literally true as the slave is actually his son.
B) Because the statement is a metaphor where 'son' implies 'free' due to the inseparable connection between sonship and freedom.
C) Because the master's intention is decisive regardless of impossibility.
D) Because the Sahibayn also agree with Abu Hanifa on this point.

Key Takeaway
The slave does not become free according to the majority Hanafi position, but Abu Hanifa's view that figurative interpretation is possible when there is a necessary relationship between terms remains an important usul principle.
